Relating to or Affecting Creditor's Rigllts. The proposed form of opinion of Bond Counsel, attached hereto as
APPENDIX D- PROPOSED FORM OF OPINION OF BOND COUNSEL, is qualified by reference to
bankruptcy, insolvency and other laws relating to or affecting creditor's rights.

                                                TAXMATIERS

         In the opinion of Sh·adling Yocca Carlson & Rauth, LLP, Newpo1t Beach, California, Bond Counsel,
under existing statutes, regulations, rulings and judicial decisions, interest (and original issue discount) on the
Bonds is excluded from gross income for federal income tax purposes and is not an item of tax preference for
purposes of calculating the federal alternative minimum tax imposed on individuals; however, it should be noted
that with respect to applicable corporations as defined in section 59(k) of the Internal Revenue Code of 1986, as
amended (the "Code"), interest (and original issue discount) with respect to the Bonds might be taken into
account in determining adjusted financial statement income for purposes of computing the alternative minimum
tax imposed on such corporations. In the further opinion of Bond Counsel, interest (and original issue discount)
on the Bonds is exempt from State of California personal income tax.

        In the opinion ofBond Counsel, the difference between the issue price of a Bond (the first price at which
a substantial amount of the Bonds of a maturity is to be sold to the public) and the stated redemption price at
maturity of such Bond constitutes original issue discount. Original issue discount accrues under a constant yield
method, and original issue discount will accrue to a Beneficial Owner before receipt of cash ath'ibutable to such
excludable income. The amount of original issue discount deemed received by a Beneficial Owner will increase
the Beneficial Owner's basis in the applicable Bond. The amount of original issue discount that accrues to the
Beneficial Owner a Bond is excluded from the gross income of such Beneficial Owner for federal income tax
purposes, is not an item of tax preference for purposes of the federal alternative minimum tax imposed on
individuals and is exempt from State of California personal income tax.

         Bond Counsel's opinion as to the exclusion from gross income for federal income tax purposes of
interest on the Bonds (including any original issue discount) is based upon certain representations of fact and
certifications made by the District, the Unde1writers and others and is subject to the condition that the Dish'ict
complies with all requirements of the Code that must be satisfied subsequent to the issuance of the Bonds to
assure that interest on the Bonds (including any original issue discount) will not become includable in gross


                                                       35
4902-2237-l 120v5/202093-000 I

                                                                                                                      P231
income for federal income tax purposes. Failure to comply with such requirements of the Code might cause
interest on the Bonds (including any original issue discount) to be included in gross income for federal income
tax purposes retroactive to the date of issuance ·o f the Bonds. The District will covenant to comply with all such
requirements.

         The amount by which a Beneficial Owner's original basis for determining loss on sale or exchange in
the applicable Bond (generally, the purchase price) exceeds the amount payable on maturity (or on an earlier call
date) constitutes amortizable bond premium, which must be am011ized under section 171 of the Code; such
amortizable bond premium reduces the Beneficial Owner's basis in the applicable Bond (and the amount oftax-
exempt interest received), and is not deductible for federal income tax purposes. The basis reduction as a result
of the amortization of bond premium may result in a Beneficial Owner realizing a taxable gain when a Bond is
sold by the Beneficial Owner for an amount equal to or less (under certain circumstances) than the original cost
of the Bond to the Beneficial Owner. Purchasers of the Bonds should consult their own tax advisors as to the
treatment, computation and collateral consequences of amo11izable bond premium.

         The Internal Revenue Service (the "IRS") has initiated an expanded program for the auditing of tax-
exempt bond issues, including both random and targeted audits. It is po~sible that the Bonds will be selected for
audit by the IRS. It is also possible that the market value of the Bonds might be affected as a result of such an
audit of the Bonds (or by an audit of similar bonds). No assurance can be given that in the course of an audit, as
a result of an audit, or otherwise, Congress or the IRS might not change the Code (or interpretation thereof)
subsequent to the issuance of the Bonds to the extent that it adversely affects the exclusion from gross income
of interest (and original issue discount) on the Bonds or their market value.

         Bond Counsel's opinion may be affected by actions taken (or not taken) or events occurring (or not

               '            · SACRAMENTO COUNTY

        The following information regarding Sacramento County is included only for the purpose ofsupplying

Sacramento County

          One of the original 27 counties in the State of California, Sacramento County was incorporated in 1850,
and Sacramento County's largest city became the State capital in 1854. With an area of approximately 994
square miles, Sacramento County is in the middle of the 400 mile long Central Valley, which is the State's
primary agricultural region. The Board of Supervisors implements a charter form ofgovernment. Five members
form the Board of Supervisors. They are elected on a non-partisan basis from supervisorial districts of
Sacramento County to four-year terms. Dishict boundaries are adjusted after every federal census to equalize
dish-ict population. Sacramento County is home to Sacramento International Airp01t, as well as several important
highways, nineteen major public and private colleges and universities, fifteen significant art and historic
museums and is renowned for its farm to fork cuisine.

                                                 APPENDIXB

                           SACRAMENTO METROPOLITAN FillE DISTRICT

History and Overview

          The Sacramento Metropolitan Fire District (the "District") was established on December 1, 2000
through the merger of the American River and Sacramento County Fire Protection Districts, which brought
together 16 predecessor fire agencies, some founded as early as the 1920s. The District is an independent special
district established under the Fire Protection District Law of 1987 (the "Fire Protection District Law"), being
Part 2.7 of Division 12 of the California Health and Safety Code. The District is the largest fire district in the
County of Sacramento ("Sacramento County"), serving a population of over 720,000 in a 359-square mile
service area that includes most of unincorporated Sacramento County, the cities of Citrus Heights and Rancho
Cordova, as well as one square mile of unincorporated area in Placer County ("Placer County" and, together
with Sacramento County, the "Counties"). Within the boundaries of the Dish'ict are wildland areas, residential
units, commercial and light indush'ial occupancies, hotels, regional hospitals, institutional buildings, local
airfields, numerous assisted living facilities, equesh·ian areas, and open space areas including hiking trails.
Several major interstate highways also h·averse the Dish'ict's jurisdiction, along with the American River, a major
recreation resource for area residents.

          The District provides all-hazard emergency response to the community it serves, including sh·uctural
and wildland fire suppression, aircraft rescue firefighting, BLS and ALS emergency medical services and
transport, ,technical rescue, hazardous materials response and mitigation, mass casualty incident response, swift
water rescue, aircraft operations, urban search and rescue, and tactical emergency medical support. In addition
to emergency response services, the District provides fire prevention and community services including building
plan review, code enforcement, arson investigation, fire and life safety programs, and public education.. The
Dish'ict responds to over 107,000 calls for service annually.

         District resources include a workforce of approximately 700 employees, a fleet of approximately 350
emergency response and support vehicles, and over 140 acres of real property, including 41 fire stations, five
support facilities, one administration building, and several future fire station sites.

         Emergency fire/EMS dispatch services are provided to the District by the Sacramento Regional
Fire/EMS Communications Center ("SRFECC"). SRFECC is a 911 Secondary PSAP (Public Safety Answering
Point) receiving approximately 350,000 phone calls per year, and dispatching over 199,000 fire and medical
emergency incidents. SRFECC provides fire/EMS dispatching for nearly all of Sacramento County and part of
Placer County, covering over 1,000 square miles and serving over 1.4 million residents. The SRFECC was
organized through a Joint Powers Agreement (JPA) in 1981 and most recently amended in 2014, to provide
public safety communications management/dispatch services for its member agencies, including the District.

Governance

        Board ofDirectors. The District is governed by a nine-member board of directors (the "Board"). Board
members are elected to four-year staggered terms, by resident voters within nine geographical divisions. The
Board is responsible for establishing District policies, adopting the annual budget, and for appointing the Fire
Chief The current members of the Board and the expiration date of their respective terms are as follows

          The Fire Chief is the Dish'ict's chief executive officer, and serves at the pleasure of the Board. The Fire
Chief is responsible for implementing Board policies, managing all aspects of the Dish·ict's fire protection and
life safety services and the overall administration of the Dish'ict. The District also employs three deputy chiefs,
seven assistant chiefs, a chief development officer, a chief human resources officer and a chief financial officer
who oversee and manage the Dish'ict's fire suppression operations, emergency medical services, community
services, fire prevention, fire investigation, finances, human resources, information technology, n·aining,
adminish·ation, facilities maintenance, logistics, and fleet repair and maintenance. Each of these positions is
appointed by and serves at the pleasure of the Fire Chief.

         Key Officers.

         Fire Chief Adam A. House. Adam A. House has served as the Fire Chief of the District since
August 2023. Chief House holds the distinction of being the first Fire Chief to serve continuously as a member
of the Dish'ict since its inception in 2000. Chief House has accumulated an impressive 36-year tenure in public
service, which includes four years in the United States Army as a firefighter. Following active duty, ChiefHouse
continued his fire service career in 1991 as a firefighter in Yuma, Arizona, and eventually promoted to the rank
of Captain. In 2000, Chiefjoined the Dish·ict, ascending the ranks from Firefighter to Captain, Battalion Chief,
and ultimately Assistant Chief before being appointed as Fire Chief.

         Chief Development Officer JeffFrve. Jeff Frye, the Dish'ict's Chief Development Officer, has been a
leader in the District for the past nine years. Mr. Frye heads the Planning and Deveiopment Division, driving
efforts in sh·ategic planning, grants administration, economic development, real estate, land use, and
intergovernmental affairs. Mr. Frye has over 20 years of prior service with the County of Sacramento, including
roles with Sacramento County Airpo11s and the County Assessor's Office.

          Chief Financial Officer Dave J. O'Toole. Dave O'Toole has served as Chief Financial Officer since
August 2021. Mr. O'Toole has worked in public finance for 24 years for both the State of California and local
public agencies. With the State of California, Mr. O'Toole last served at the Chief Operating Officer of the State
Conh·oller's Office, overseeing statewide financial oversight and reporting, and formulating debt management
sn·ategies for the State. While serving as Chief Financial Officer of the Dish·ict, Mr. O'Toole has managed the
Disn·ict through the financial pressures of the COVID-19 pandemic era and his team earned the Government
Finance Officers ofAmerica "Triple Crown" award for the District's annual financial report, community annual
report, and District budget. Mr. O'Toole is an active member of the California Society of Municipal Finance
Officers association and serves as chair of the Sacramento Valley chapter.

Accounting and Financial Reporting

         The financial statements of the Dish'ict are prepared in conformity with generally accepted accounting
principles ("GAAP") for governmental units. The Governmental Accounting Standards Board ("GASB") is the
standard-setting body for establishing governmental accounting and financial reporting principles.

       Management has in place a comprehensive framework of internal conh·qls within the organization.
These controls are policies and procedures put in place to safeguard assets from misappropriation, to assure
management authorizes all transactions, and to verify proper recording and reporting of transactions using
GAAP.

         The accounting and financial reporting treatment is determined by the applicable measurement focus
and basis of accounting. Measurement focus indicates the type of resources being measured such as current
financial resources or economic resources. The basis of accounting indicates the timing ofh·ansactions or events
for recognition in the financial statements. Governmental fund financial statements are reported using the current
financial resources measurement focus and the modified accrual basis of accounting. Revenues are recognized
as soon as they are both measurable and available. Revenues are considered to be available when they are
collectible within the current period or soon enough thereafter to pay liabilities of the current period. The District
considers all revenues, except property taxes, to be available if they are collected within 90 days of the end of
the current fiscal period. Propetty taxes are recognized if received within 60 days of the end of the current fiscal
period.

        Propetty taxes, charges for services, intergovernmental revenues, rental income, and investment
earnings associated with the current fiscal period are all considered to be susceptible to accrual and so have been
recognized as revenues of the current fiscal period. Expenditure-driven grants are recognized as revenue when
the qualifying expenditures have been incurred and all other eligibility requirements have been met, and the
amount is received during the period or within the availability period for this revenue source. All other revenue
items are considered to be measurable and available only when cash is received by the Dish'ict.

         Expenditures generally are recorded when a liability is incurred, as under accrual accounting. However,
debt service expenditures, as well as expenditures related to compensated absences and workers compensation
claims, are recorded only when payment is due. General capital asset acquisitions, including entering into
contracts granting the District the right to use lease assets and information technology software, are reported as



                                                         B-4
4902-2237-l 120v5/202093-000 I


                                                                                                                         P243
expenditures in governmental funds. Issuance of long-te1m debt and fmancing through leases and subscription-
based information technology arrangements are reported as other financing sources.

         The accounts of the Distl'ict are organized on the basis of funds. A fund is a separate accounting unit
with a self-balancing set of accounts. The operations of each fund are accounted for with a separate set of self-
balancing accounts that comprise its assets, liabilities, fund equity, revenues, and expenditures. District
resources are allocated to and accounted for in individual funds based upon the purpose for which they are to be
spent. The emphasis of fund financial statements is on major governmental funds reported as separate columns
in the fund financial statements. All remaining governmental funds are aggregated and rep01ted as nonmajor
funds. The District reports its General Fund as a major governmental fund, and the General Fund is used to
account for all financial resources except those required or designated by the Board to be accounted for in another
fund. The General Fund is the operating fund of the District and is generally used to account for all financial
resources relative to operations.

        The District maintains a professional fmance staff that performs among other things, budgeting,
fmancial planning, financial reporting, payroll, procurement, grant accounting, and financial transactions
processing. District staff processes payroll checks in-house while vendor claims are paid by the Sacramento
County acting as the District's fiscal agent. District revenues are deposited in the Sacramento County Pooled
Investment Fund.

        Dish·ict officials approve all vendor claims for payment and submit them to Sacramento County Auditor-
Controller, which verifies the amounts are within the adopted budget. No amounts exceeding the authorized
budgeted amounts will be paid by Sacramento County Auditor-Conh·oller.

        The District's audited financial statements for fiscal year 2023-24 along with accompanying notes and
opinions from the Auditor are set forth in APPENDIX C- ANNUAL COMPREHENSIVE FINANCIAL
REPORT OF THE DISTRICT FOR THE FISCAL YEAR ENDED JUNE 30, 2024. The Dish·ict's fiscal year
2023-24 Annual Comprehensive Financial Report has been recognized with the Government Finance Officers
of America ("GFOA") award for excellence in financial repo1ting, the ninth consecutive year the District has
received this award.

Budget Procedure, Current Budget and Historical Budget Information

         General. The District adopts annual budgets on a basis consistent with GAAP for the General Fund,
Special Projects Fund, Capital Facilities Fund, Grant Fund and Impact Fee Fund. As required by the laws of the
State, a preliminary and a final budget are adopted on or before July 1 and October 1 of each fiscal year,
respectively. Significant operating changes are addressed by the Board in their Midyear Budget amendment,
which is typically adopted in March. The appropriated budget is prepared by fund, division, and object level.

         The legal level of budgetary conh·ol is at the fund level and then the object level. Each fund will have
its own budget for the following items: (I) salaries and benefits, (2) services and supplies, (3) capital outlay, and
(4) debt service. The Board must approve amendments or transfers of appropriations between funds or objects.

          Appropriations in all budgeted funds lapse at the end of the fiscal year even if they have related
encumbrances. Encumbrances are conunitrnents related to unperformed (executory) contracts for goods or
services (i.e., purchase orders, conh·acts, and commitments). Encumbrance accounting is utilized to the extent
necessary to assure effective budgetary control and accountability and to facilitate effective cash planning and
control. While all appropriations and encumbrances lapse at year end, valid outstanding encumbrances (those
for which performance under the executory contract is expected in the next year) are re-appropriated and become
part of the subsequent year's budget.

        For budgeting purposes, the Dish-ict accounts for the activities of its leased properties and IGT (as
defined herein) activities separately from its General Fund activities. However, for purposes of this Official


                                                        B-5
4902-2237-1120v5/202093-000I


                                                                                                                        P244
Statement and consistent with the presentation in the audited financial statements, those activities have been
combined in the General Fund.

          Fiscal Year 2024-25 Final Budget. The District's fiscal year 2024-25 Final Budget (the "2024-25 Final
Budget") was adopted by the Board on September 12, 2024 and later amended at midyear on March 13, 2025.
The 2024-25 Final Budget includes approximately $326.6 million in General Fund revenues, which represents
approximately $16.8 million (5.4%) increase relative to fiscal year 2023-24 actuals, and approximately $328.9
million in expenditures, which represents an approximately $36.6 million (12.5%) increase in expenditures
relative to fiscal year 2023-24 actuals. After accounting for operating transfers, the 2024-25 Final Budget results
in revenues and financing sources $9.1 million less than expenditures, which are expected to be covered by a
reduction in General Fund reserves.

        The amount of General Fund reserve is projected in the 2024-25 Final Budget to be $39.6 million on
June 30, 2025, a $9.1 million reduction from June 30, 2024. As a percentage of net budgeted expenditures in
the 2024-25 Final Budget, this is expected to be an 11.9% reserve. The reserve decline is mainly attributable to
slowing property tax growth, employee contract commitments, and financial market conditions. As specified in
the District's reserve policy, any unassigned fund balance remaining at fiscal year-end will be classified as
General Fund reserves and included in the reserve amount.

          The District has been recognized by the GFOA with their excellence in budget reporting award for the
last three fiscal years. With the two financial reporting awards previously described, the District realized "Triple
Crown" recognition by the GFOA; recognition achieved by less than half of one percent of public agencies in
the U.S. and Canada. Additionally, the Dish·ict has been recognized with the California Society of Municipal
Finance Officers' budgeting excellence award for the last three fiscal years.

        Fiscal Year 2025-26 Pre/iminmy B udget. The District is currently preparing its fiscal year 2025-26
Prelimina1y Budget and will present it to the Board on June 12, 2025. The Board will adopt the 2025-26
Preliminary Budget before July 1, 2025. Modifications to the budget will be adopted by the Board in September
2025, as part of the 2024-25 Final Budget.

         The District's fiscal year 2025-26 Preliminary Budget forecasts a General Fund shortfall of$4.8 million,
based mainly on slower than expected property tax and EMS growth. The District will have a clearer picture of
the fiscal year 2024- 25 budget when it presents its Final Budget in August 2025. The Final Budget will include
carryover savings and unspent fund balances, which typically increase the size of the reserve. Based on prior
year revenue trends at Final Budget, the District expects to eliminate or significantly reduce the size of the
General Fund shortfal l.

Financial Policies

         Fi11a11cial Plmmi11g a11d Budget Policy. The District's Financial Planning and Budget Policy
memorializes the District's major budget-related processes, including the District's definition of a balanced
budget, the process for developing the Preliminary, Final, and Midyear Budgets, use of one- time revenues,
timing to recognize prior year expenditures and revenues after fiscal year end, direction for spending excess
revenues after the 15 percent reserve target has been met, and the calculation for the General Fund reserve. After
the 15 percent reserve ratio is achieved, the remaining surplus is distributed with 50 percent allocated for capital
projects and 50 percent allocated for payment of the District's unfunded pension and OPEB liabilities.

        Resen1e F1111di11g Policy. The District's Reserve Funding Policy targets a General Fund operating
reserve of 1.8 months (or approximately 15%) of general fund operating expenditures. The District is able to
operate with this minimum amount due to a County "Dry Period Funding" credit line used in anticipation of
property tax receipts (as described under the caption "-Sho1t Term Obligations"). Under the District's Reserve
Funding Policy, the maximum amount of General Fund operating reserves is six months (or 50%) of general
fund operating expenditures. Such maximum amount would allow the District the cash flow needed to operate
without Sacramento County "Dry Period Funding" credit line.



                                                           B-8
4902-2237-1I20v5/202093-0001


                                                                                                                       P247
         Capital Asset Policy. The District's Capital Asset Policy defines capital assets as land, buildings,
equipment, and other related improvements with an individual cost of$5,000 or more and a useful life of at least
one year. The policy establishes a capital asset accounting system that provides guidelines for the accountability
and financial and physical control of capital assets, consistent and uniform procedures and transactions for
accounting of capital assets, and compliance with funding entity requirements for grant-funded assets. Board
approval is required for budgeted capital asset acquisitions and for disposal of capital assets. This policy also
requires a biennial invent01y of all capital assets.

          Capital Improvement Program Policy. The Dish·ict's Capital Improvement Program Policy organizes,
facilitates, and memorializes capital needs and goals in order to efficiently and transparently develop and support
the physical infrastructure of the District. Rigorous and methodical evaluation of facility, apparatus, and
equipment needs and financing options ensures that existing and future capital needs are met, in compliance with
the District's sh·ategic plan and financial resources. The development of the CIP is a coordinated effo1t across
the District, with the capital project planning process overseen by the Planning and Development Division and
the finance and budget process elements overseen by the Finance Division.

         Debt Management Policy. The Dish·ict's Debt Management Policy sets forth written guidelines for the
issuance, structuring, and administration of debt issued to finance District expenditures. This policy prohibits
the issuance of long-term debt to fund District operating costs, as well as the use of any derivative products.
Debt structuring provisions include a requirement that the maturity of a debt issue be consistent with the
economic or useful life of the asset being financed, generally level annual debt services payments, and
preferences for fixed interest rates and optional redemption at a reasonable call date.

          Pension Funding Bonds Early Payoff Policy. The Pension Funding Bonds Early Payoff Policy
establishes the framework for ensuring that the District's Pension Obligation Bonds are paid off in a manner
designed to minimize interest costs and provides a mechanism for paying off the bonds at the earliest possible
dates. This policy requires annual deposits with the h·ustee for the Pension Obligation Bonds such that these
amounts, together with investment earnings, will be sufficient to retire the 2004B Pension Obligation Bonds on
their first optional call date ofNovember 19, 2025. For more information regarding the Dish·ict's outstanding
Pension Obligation Bonds, see the caption "- Long-Term Obligations."

         Post-Retirement Medical Reserves. The Dish'ict has been pre-funding its retiree medical obligations
since 2013 and the Dish·ict's 2024-25 Final Budget reflects contributions of approximately $4.2 million. This
conh·ibution will fund the explicit subsidy liability, budgeted as a combination of retiree medical premium
payments and contributions to the California Employers' Retiree Benefit Trust Fund. After eleven years of such
pre-funding, the unfunded liability is approximately 26 percent funded. For more information regarding the
Dish'ict's OPEB Plan see the caption "-Other Postemployment Benefits."

Capital Improvement Program

         The purpose of the Dish·ict's Capital Improvement Program ("CIP") is to organize, facilitate, and
memorialize capital needs and goals in order to efficiently and transparently develop and support the physical
infrastructure of the District. The CIP incorporates and is consistent with Dish·ict master plans, the Board's goals,
and other long-range plans of the District, and serves as a planning tool that identifies and plans for the funding
of capital projects in order to ensure timely acquisition and replacement of needed facilities, improvements,
apparatus, and equipment. It is the policy of the District to prepare a Capital Improvement Program Plan ("CIP
Plan") annually for adoption by the Board, which represents the District's known and anticipated capital needs
over the next five years. The CIP Plan also includes a one-year CIP Budget ("CIP Budget"), which is adopted as
part of the District's annual budget process. The District's fiscal year 2024-25 CIP Plan identified 56 capital
projects totaling approximately $182 million through fiscal year 2028-29, including approximately $ 13 .8 million
in new capita l projects funded in fiscal year 2024-25 and approximately $38.0 million in carryover projects from
fiscal year 2023-24. The District's fiscal year 2025-26 Plan identified 73 capital projects totaling approximately
$350 million through fiscal year 2029-30, and will be adopted by the Board in June 2025.

   Major Revenue Sources (Fund Basis)

           General. For fiscal year 2023-24, properly tax revenues collected by Sacramento County and Placer
   County and allocated to the District represented approxjmately 65.6% if the District's General Fund revenues
   and charges for services represented approximately 31.8% of the District's General Fund revenues. For fiscal
   year 2024-25, the District has budgeted for property tax revenues to amount to approximately 65.3% of General
   Fund revenues and for charges for services amounting to approximately 31.8% of General Fund revenues. The
   remainder of the Dish'ict's revenues generally consist of ce1tain intergovernmental revenues, investment income
   and other miscellaneous revenues.

           Property Taxes. Prope1ty tax revenues collected by Sacramento County and Placer County and
  allocated to the District represent the Disn·ict's largest revenue source, which amounted to approximately $203 .3


                                                                  B-11
  4902-2237- I 120v5/202093-000 I


                                                                                                                                            P250
million. In its Fiscal Year 2024-25 Final Budget, the District has budgeted for property tax revenues amounting
to $213.5 million.

        Sacramento County and Placer County levy property taxes on behalf of the District for the District's
general operating purposes as well as for the payment of voter-approved general obligation bonds, such as the
Bonds. For more information regarding the property taxation system in California, as well certain historical
information regarding assessed valuations within the District's service area, see the caption "SECURITY AND
SOURCES OF PAYMENT FOR THE BONDS" in this Official Statement.

       Chargesfor Sen1ices. Charges for services represent the District's second largest revenue source, which
amounted to $98.4 million in fiscal year 2023-24. In its Fiscal Year 2024-25 Final Budget, the Dish·ict has
budgeted for charges for services amounting to approximately $103.9 million.

         Charges for services represent various cost reimbursements that the District obtains in the normal course
of its operations. The bulk of these charges comes from cost recoveries for the District's emergency medical
transports. In addition, the District obtains reimbursements from other agencies for costs associated with
deploying personnel and equipment to respond to emergencies. These reimbursements are reflected as revenues
and the related costs as expenses.                                                           •

        The District provides the bulk of ambulance service through its fire stations and conh·acts with a private
ambulance service company, American Medical Response ("AMR"), to augment its services during times of
high demand. Approximately 67% of the total charges for ambulance service calls received by the District are
covered by Medicare and MediCal fee schedules and approximately 13% are covered by private insurance with
the remainder paid by the patient. In fiscal year 2023-24, ambulance service revenues equaled approximately
$47 million, or 6.8% more than the previous year.

         The Dish·ict retains the services of a professional ambulance billing and consulting services firm to
manage the billing and collections of the ambulance transport revenues. Presently, the Dish-ict's net collection
rate averages about 90% of net charges billed (total charges billed less contractual write downs for Medicare,
Medical, and other insurances). In fiscal year 2023-24, net charges billed were $85 million. Delinquent accounts
that resulted in write offs averaged 8% of the net charges billed in the last fiscal year. The District also employs
the services of a collection agency for those accounts that have the potential of recovery, but require more
aggressive collection activities.

         The Dish·ict also participates in a statewide Intergovernmental Transfer ("IGT") program by which the
federal government provides for federal matching funds related to the medical assessment and transportation of
Medi-Cal managed care beneficiaries. There are two IGT programs: Voluntary Rate Range Program ("VRRP")
and Public Provider Ground Emergency Medical Transport ("PP-GEMT"). VRRP requires the Dish·ict to
transfer funds to the State, which then receives matching funds from the federal govermnent. The Stat~ then
transfer the funds, less its adminish·ative fees, to the managed care plans which then pay the District. PP-GEMT
also requires the District to h·ansfer funds to the State in return for add-on increase to reimbursements for eligible
emergency medical h·ansports. The Districts records the gross amount h·ansferred to the State as expenses and
the gross amount received as revenues. In fiscal year 2023-24, in addition to ambulance revenues previously
noted, PP-GEMT gross revenues amounted to approximately $20.9 million while VRRP revenues amounted to
approximately $15.0 million.

         Other District R evenues. The District also receives intergovernmental revenues, which generally
consist of residual and pass-through property tax revenues dish·ibuted by with respect to former redevelopment
agencies, interest income, and other miscellaneous revenues which generally consist of grants, insurance




                                                       B-12
4902-2237-I 120v5/202093-0001


                                                                                                                         P251
proceeds, and an administrative fee received by the District for its role as the administrator of the predecessor
program to the PP-GEMT program.

Long-Term Obligations

         General. The following is a brief summary of the District's long-term obligations. For more
information on the Dish·ict's long term liabilities, see Note 5 in the District's audited financial statements for
fiscal year 2023-24 attached to this Official Statement as APPENDIX C- ANNUAL COMPREHENSNE
FINANCIAL REPORT OF THE DISTRICT FOR THE FISCAL YEAR ENDED JUNE 30, 2024.

         Pension Bonds. In October 2004, the District issued taxable pension obligation bonds in three series
for the original amount of $69,998,975 (the "Pension Obligation Bonds"). The proceeds of the Pension
Obligation Bonds were used to reduce the unfunded actuarial liabilities of its pension plans. Series A bonds
were issued as fixed rate bonds which require semi-annual payments of interest at a fixed rate of 4.79% with
annual principal payments that began on May 15, 2005 until the final maturity date of May 15, 2025. Series B
bonds were issued as auction rate securities which initially require semi-annual interest payments at a rate of
5.37% which will reset to auction rates on November 19, 2025 with principal payments beginning on
May 15, 2026 until the final maturity date ofMay 15, 2030. By policy, the Dish·ict is setting aside funds annually
for the purpose of fully retiring the Series B bonds on their initial auction date. As of June 30, 2024, the Pension
Obligation Bonds were outstanding in the aggregate principal amount of$22,935,000.

        Lease Revenue Bonds. On February 24, 2022, the Dish·ict issued 2022 Lease Revenue Refunding
Bonds (the "2022 Bonds") with an annual interest rate of2.53% and par value of$8,030,000. The proceeds of
the bond issuance were used for the early redemption of the Dish·ict's 2011 Lease Revenue Bonds. The 2022
Bonds have a final maturity of January 1, 2040. As of June 30, 2024, the 2022 Bonds were outstanding in the
aggregate principal amount of$7,277,000.

         Equipment Financing Agreement. The District has entered into equipment financing agreements with
certain banks under which the related asset will become the property of the District when all payment terms of
the contract are met. The District's outstanding equipment financings have terms that expire from 2025 through
2029 with interest rates ranging from 0.918% to 4.321%. As of June 30, 2024, notes from direct borrowings
were outstanding in the aggregate principal amount of$13,033,259.

Short Term Borrowings

         The District has borrowings available from Sacramento County equal to 85% of estimated tax revenues
for July 1 to December 10 and 42.50% of estimated revenues for December 10 to April 25. These borrowings
are permitted until the last Monday ofApril each year. The amount available as ofDecember 10, 2023 and April
25, 2024 were $167,229,517 and $83,614,759, respectively. The interest rate charged is the rate earned by
Sacramento County investment pool which was 4.6719% for fiscal year 2023-24. During fiscal year 2023-24,
the District borrowed and repaid $161,692,107 under such program. During fiscal year 2024-25 to date, the
District has - - - - -

Risk Management

         General. The District is exposed to various risks of loss related to torts, theft of, damage to and
destruction of assets, errors and omission, injuries to employees, and natural disasters. The Dish·ict manages
these risks through various full insurance policies and self-insurance combined with excess insurance policies.

        Workers' Compensation. The Dish·ict is self-insured for all losses from workers' compensation claims
from 2003, when it terminated its excess liability insurance coverage, until 2009. In 2010, the District obtained
excess commercial coverage for losses in excess of $3,000,000 for each claim up to the statutory limits.



                                                      B-13
4902-2237-l 12Ov5/202093-000 I


                                                                                                                       P252
Beginning in fiscal year 2023-24, excess insurance covered losses in exces.s of $4,000,000 for each claim up to
the statutory limits.

         Other Coverages. The District is a member of the Fire Agencies Risk Insurance Authority ("FAIRA").
FAIRA is a risk- pooling self-insurance authority, created under the provisions of the California Government
Code Sections 6500 et. seq. and its purpose is to provide a full risk management program for fire and emergency
service agencies. The District pays an annual premium to FAIRA for general and auto liability, property, boiler
and machinery, and employee dishonesty insurance coverage. The District's annual premium is based on its pro-
rata share of charges for the pooled risk, claims adjusting and legal costs, and adminish·ative and other costs to
operate FAmA. Aviation, cyber, and pollution insurance continues to be covered through a commercial insurer.

        The District's coverage and corresponding deductibles are as follows:

                                  Coverage                    Amount            Deductible
                   General liability                        $ 1,000,000          $50,000
                   Auto liability                             1,000,000           50,000
                   Property damage                           10,000,000           50,000
                   Boiler and machinery                      10,000,000           50,000
                   Employment practices liability             1,000,000           50,000
                   Employee dishonesty                        2,000,000           50,000
                   Aviation Liability                        10,000,000
                   Cyber                                      5,000,000            75,000
                   Pollution                                  1,000,000            10,000
                   Fiduciary                                  5,000,000            10,000

         For claims exceeding the above coverages, FAmA obtained excess pool insurance for liability claims
up to $ 11 .5 million subject to certain aggregate limits and excess pool insurance for property damages up to
$100 million. In addition, The District purchased two separate $5 million excess insurance policies to extend its
excess liability coverage to $21.5 million. Meh·o Fire did not have any settlements in excess of the insurance
coverage in any of the three prior fiscal years.

       For more information regarding the District's risk management practices, see Note 8 in the District's
audited financial statement attached to this Official Statement as APPENDIX C-                  ANNUAL
COMPREHENSIVE FINANCIAL REPORT OF THE DISTRICT FOR THE FISCAL YEAR ENDED
JUNE 30, 2024.

Retirement Plans

         Plan Descriptions. The Dish·ict provides pension benefits to eligible employees through defined benefit
pension plans administered by to the California Public Employees' Retirement System ("CalPERS") and the
Sacramento County Employees' Retirement System ("SCERS"), which both act as a common investment and
administrative agent for its pa1ticipating member employers. Benefit provisions and all other requirements for
CalPERS are established by State statute and benefit provisions and all other requirements for SCERS are
established by Section 31584 of Sacramento County Employees Retirement Law of 1937. The establishment
and amendment of specific benefit provisions of the plans are authorized by resolutions of the Board.

        The District conh'ibutes to the following defined benefit pension plans:

    •   The Safety Plan of the Sacramento Meh·opolitan Fire Dish·ict (the "Safety Plan"), an agent multiple-
        employer defined benefit pension plan administered by CalPERS.



                                                     B-14
4902-2237-1120v5/202093-000J


                                                                                                                     P253
    •   The Miscellaneous Plan of the Sacramento Metropolitan Fire District (the "Miscellaneous Plan"), a cost-
        sharing multiple-employer defined benefit pension plan administered by CalPERS.

    •   The Sacramento County Employees Retirement System Plan (the "SCERS Plan"), a cost-sharing
        multiple-employer defined benefit pension plan administered by Sacramento County.

         Only the plans administered by CalPERS are open for new emollment. SCERS is only responsible for
providing benefits to employees or retirees of the Dish'ict who were members ofSCERS before January l, 1997.
CalPERS issues publicly available financial reports for its plans. Copies of the CalPERS annual financial report
and pertinent past trend information may be obtained at https://www.calpers.ca.gov. SCERS issues a publicly
available financial report for its plans. Copies of the SCERS annual report may be obtained at
http://www.scers.org.

         Benefits Provided. Each plan provides retirement and disability benefits, annual cost-of-living
adjustments and death benefits to plan members and beneficiaries. The passage of the Public Employees'
Pension Reform Act ("PEPRA") imposed several resh·ictions to the pension benefits that may be offered to
employees hired on or after January 1, 2013 that were not previously CalPERS members, including increasing
1ninimum retirement ages, increasing the percentage required for member contributions, and excluding certain
types of compensation as pensionable. PEPRA has also created limits on pensionable compensation tied to the
Social Security taxable wage base. The cumulative effect of these PEPRA changes will ultimately reduce the
District's retirement costs.

         All permanent full-time employees and part-time employees working at least 1,000 hours per year are
enrolled in the CalPERS Safety Plan or the Miscellaneous Plan. Safety membership is extended only to those
involved in fire suppression, and certain other related classifications, while all other employees are classified as
Miscellaneous. Upon retirement, paiiicipants are entitled to a monthly retirement benefit, payable for life, in an
amount equal to a benefit factor multiplied by their service years and by their highest average monthly salruy
over 12 consecutive months of employment or, in the case of PEPRA employees, 36 consecutive months of
employment.

         The Plans' provisions and benefits in effect at June 30, 2024 are summarized as follows:

          Co11tributio11s. Section 20814(c) of the California Public Employees' Retirement Law ("PERL")
requires that the employer contribution rates for all public employers be determined on an annual basis by the
actuary and shall be effective on the July 1 following notice of a change in the rate. For each of the CalPERS
plans, the contractually required contribution rate was a specified percent of annual payroll, actuarially
determined as an amount that, when combined with employee contributions, is expected to finance the costs of
benefits earned by employees during the year; and a fixed dollar amount contribution for any unfunded accrued
liability for past service.

         ContTibution rates for the Safety Plan are individually determined for the District by CalPERS and do
not reflect any cost-sharing provisions. Contribution rates for the Miscellaneous Plan are determined with
reference to the risk pool to which they belong. Additionally, CalPERS manages a side fund for the
Miscellaneous Plan, that was created at the time the District joined the plan, to reflect the difference between the
funded status of the District's plan and the funded status of the risk pool. The side fund is invested and is being
amortized to reduce the normal required contributions.

        Required normal cost contributions were made each pay period based on required contribution rates as
shown below. Unfunded liability contributions were made in July 2023 for Miscellaneous Classic and PEPRA
separately and for Safety Classic and PEPRA combined. In addition to their employee contributions, classic
safety employees contribute 3% of their pay towards employer conh'ibutions.

           The net pension liability for CalPERS Miscellaneous Plan is measured as the prop011ionate share of
  the collective net pension liability of the Miscellaneous Risk Pool (risk pool) as a whole. In determining an
  individual rate plan's proportion of the risk pool's net pension liability, CalPERS first estimates the total
  pension liability and the fiduciary net position for the risk pool as a whole as well as the individual rate plans.
  The allocated net pension liability represents the difference between the plan's proportionate share of the
  total pension liability and fiduciary net position. Metro Fi.re's proportionate share of the net pension liability
  for the Miscellaneous Plan is as follows:

                                       Proportion - June 30, 2023               0.43995%
                                       Proportion - June 30, 2024               0.44277%
                                       Change - Increase (Decrease)             0.00282%

        As the District has withdrawn from SCERS, SCERS calculates the net pension liability in a separate
account as contractually agreed. Although a separate study is done to determine the Dish-ict's total pension


                                                              B-18
4902-2237-I I 20v5/202093-000 I


                                                                                                                                   P257
liability and fiduciary net position, the study uses assumptions based on the combined experience ofthe risk pool
as a whole (e.g., longevity, salary increases, etc.).

          On June 25, 2012, GASB approved GASB Statement No. 68 ("GASB 68") with respect to pension
accounting and financial repo11ing standards for state and local governments and pension plans. GASB 68 states
that, for pensions within the scope of the statement, a cost-sharing employer that does not have a special funding
situation is required to recognize a net pension liability, deferred outflows of resources, deferred inflows of
resources related to pensions, and pension expense based on its proportionate share of the net pension liability
for benefits provided through the pension plan. While the new accounting standards change financial statement
reporting requirements, they do not impact funding policies of the pension systems. The audited financial
statements of the District for fiscal years 2018-19 and thereafter reflect the application of the GASB 68. GASB
68 is a change in accounting repo11ing standards but it does not change the District pension plan funding
obligations.

           Pension Expenses. . Pension expense represents the change in the net pension liability during the
  measurement period, adjusted for actual contributions and the deferred recognition of changes in inveshnent
  gain/Joss, achlarial gain/Joss, actuarial assumptions or method, and plan benefits. For the year ended June
  30, 2023, the Dish·ict recognized pension expense as follows:

                CalPERS Safety Plan                                      $ 75,305,521
                Ca!PERS Miscellaneous Plan                                  3,857,076
                SCERS Plan                                                  1,766,459
                   Total Pension Expense                                 $ 80,929,056

           Discount Rate. The long-term expected rate of rehlrn on pension plan inveshnents was determined
  using a building-block method in which expected future real rates of return (expected returns, net of pension
  plan investment expense and inflation) are developed for each major asset class.

          In determining the long-term expected rate of return, CalPERS took into account both short-term
  and long-term market return expectations. Using historical returns of all of the funds' asset classes, expected
  compound (geometric) returns were calculated over the next 20 years using a building-block approach. The
  expected rate ofrehlrn was then adjusted to account for assumed administrative expenses of 10 basis points.

Other Postemployment Benefits

         Plan Description. The Sacramento Metropolitan Fire District Retiree Healthcare Plan ("OPEB Plan")
is a single-employer defined benefit healthcare plan administered by the District. The OPEB Plan provides
healthcare benefits to eligible retirees and their dependents through CalPERS under the ,Public Employees'
Medical and Hospital Care Act ("PEMHCA"). This coverage requires the employee to satisfy the requirements
for retirement: (a) attainment of age 50 (age 52, for miscellaneous employees who are new to CalPERS on or
after January 1, 20 13) with five years of State or public agency service or (b) an approved disability retirement.

         Benefits Provided. Benefit provisions are established and may be amended tluough agreements and
memorandums of understanding among the District, its non-represented employees, and the unions for
represented District employees. As a PEMHCA employer, the District is obligated to contribute toward the cost
of retiree medical coverage for the retiree's lifetime or until coverage is discontinued. The District has
resolutions with CalPERS defining the level of contribution toward active and retiree medical plan premiums.
Benefits fall into one of four categories:

       1.     For employees hired prior to December 1, 2011, the current resolutions provide that: (a) while
              covered by a Basic or Combination plan, the District will contribute 92% of the medical premium
              for the retiree and his or her dependents, but not more than 92% of the premium for the 3rd highest
              Basic or Combination plan rate corresponding to the retiree's coverage level, and (b) when the
              retiree and all of his or her covered dependents are covered by Medicare plans, Metro Fire will
              contribute 100% of the medical premium, not to exceed 100% of the 3rd highest Supplemental plan
              rate corresponding to the retiree's coverage level.

       2.     Employees hired on or after December l, 2011 in general are covered by a "special" PEMHCA
              vesting resolution, which provides that the District will pay the benefit described in 1 above,
              multiplied by a vesting percentage with a minimum service of 5 years starting at 25% increasing by
              intervals of 5% with full vesting to those having 20 years of service.

       3.     Retirees who retain SCERS medical coverage receive a subsidy, increasing by 5% per year, based
              on the retiree's years ofservice with the District. The benefit continues at 50% for surviving spouses
              of retirees.

       4.     The Dish·ict also reimburses the monthly Medicare Part B premiums for former employees who
              retired from American River Fire Dish·ict and their spouses.

         All retirees eligible for coverage under the district-sponsored medical plans (except those described in
paragraph 3 above) who opt out ofCalPERS medical insurance will receive instead up to $300 per month toward
the cost of outside coverage.

       Employees Covered. Based on the most recent actuarial information available, the following employees
were covered by the benefit terms of the OPEB Plan:

       Inactive or their beneficiaries currently receiving benefits                                        750
       Inactive entitled to but not yet receiving benefits                                                  13

       Total covered employees                                                                           1,457

         Contributio11s. The contribution requirements of the OPEB Plan participants and the District are
established by and may be amended pursuant to agreements with its non-represented employees and the union
for represented District employees. The Dish·ict prefunds its obligation by conh·ibuting to the California


                                                           B-21
4902-2237-1120v5/202093-000 I


                                                                                                                       P260
Employer's Retiree Benefit Trust ("CERBT") Fund, an irrevocable trust established to fund post-employment
medical benefits. The District has conunitted more than $63.1 million to the CERBT fund since establishing the
trust fund in 2013. Combined with investment earnings, the District's CERBT balance was approximately $93.4
million as of February 2025, improving the District's overall OPEB funding level. CERBT is administrated by
CalPERS, and is managed by an appointed board not under the control ofthe District. CalPERS issues a publicly
available financial report for CERBT that can be obtained at www.calpers.ca.gov/page/employerslbenefit-
programs/cerbt.

        In accordance with its annual budget, the Dish'ict contributes to CERBT an amount that, together with
the payment of retiree medical premiums, will fully fund the explicit portion of the actuarially determined
conh·ibution and will fund the implicit subsidy liability on a pay-as-you-go basis. An "implicit subsidy" exists
when the premiums charged for retiree coverage are lower than the expected retiree claims for that coverage.
Pre-Medicare retirees able to continue medical coverage at the same premium rates as are charged for active
employees creates this implicit benefit. Employees are not required to conh·ibute to the OPEB Plan.

                                 DTC AND THE BOOK-ENTRY ONLY SYSTEM


         The following description of the procedures and record keeping with respect to beneficial ownership
interests in the Bonds, payment of principal of and interest on the Bonds to Direct Participants, Indirect
Participants or Beneficial Owners (as such terms are defined below) ofthe Bonds, confirmation and hw1sfer of
beneficial ownership interests in the Bonds and other Bond related h·ansactions by and between DTC, Direct
Participants, Indirect Participants and Beneficial Owners ofthe Bonds is based solely on info rmation furnished
by DTC to the Dish'ict which the District believes to be reliable, but the Dish·ict and the Undenvriter do not and
cannot make any independent representations concerning these 111atters and do not take responsibility for the
accuracy or completeness thereof Neither the DTC, Direct Participants, Indirect Participants nor the Beneficial
Owners should rely on the foregoing information with respect to such mallers but should instead confirm the
same with DTC or the DTC Participants, as the case 111ay be.

         The Depository Trust Company ("DTC"), New York, New York, will act as securities depository for the
Bonds. The Bonds will be issued as fully registered securities registered in the name of Cede & Co. (DTC's
partnership nominee) or such other name as may be requested by an authorized representative ofDTC. One fully
registered Bond will be issued for each maturity of the Bonds, each in the aggregate principal amount of such
maturity, and will be deposited with DTC.

         DTC, the world's largest securities depository, is a limited-purpose trust company organized under the
New York Banking Law, a "banking organization" within the meaning of the New York Banking Law, a member
of the Federal Reserve System, a "clearing corporation" within the meaning of the New York Uniform
Commercial Code, and a "clearing agency" registered pursuant to the provisions of Section 17A of the Securities
Exchange Act of 1934. DTC holds and provides asset servicing for over 3.5 million issues of U.S. and non-U.S.
equity issues, corporate and municipal debt issues and money market instruments (from over 100 countries) that
DTC's participants ("Direct Pai1icipants") deposit with DTC. DTC also facilitates the post-trade settlement
among Direct Participants of sales and other securities transactions in deposited securities, tlu-ough elech·onic
computerized book-enh-y transfers and pledges between Direct Participants' accounts. This eliminates the need
for physical movement of securities ce11ificates. Direct Participants include both U.S. and non-U.S. securities
brokers and dealers, banks, h·ust companies, clearing corporations, and certain other organizations. DTC is a
wholly owned subsidia1-y of The Depository Trust & Clearing Corporation ("DTCC"). DTCC is the holding
company for DTC, National Securities Clearing Corporation and Fixed Income Cleating Corporation all of
which are registered clearing agencies. DTCC is owned by the users of its regulated subsidiaries. Access to the
DTC system is also available to others such as both U.S. and non-U.S. securities brokers and dealers, banks,
trust companies and clearing corporations that clear through or maintain a custodial relationship with a Direct
Pa11icipant, either directly or indirectly ("Indirect Participants"). DTC has a Standard & Poor's rating of AA+.
The DTC Rules applicable to its Participants are on file with the Securities and Exchange Commission. More
information about DTC can be found at www.dtcc.com and www.dtc.org.

         Purchases of Bonds under the DTC system must be made by or tlu·ough Direct Pai1icipants, which will
receive a credit for the Bonds on DTC's records. The ownership interest of each actual purchaser of each Bond
("Beneficial Owner") is in turn to be recorded on the Direct and Indirect Participants' records. Beneficial Owners
will not receive written confirmation from DTC of their purchase. Beneficial Owners are, however, expected to
receive written confirmations providing details of the h·ansaction, as well as periodic statements oftheir holdings,
from the Direct or Indirect Participant tlu-ough which the Beneficial Owner entered into the transaction. Transfers
of ownership interests in the Bonds are to be accomplished by enh·ies made on the books of Direct and Indirect
Participants acting on behalf of Beneficial Owners. Beneficial Owners will not receive certificates representing
their ownership interests in the Bonds, except in the event that use of the book-entry system for the Bonds is
discontinued.



                                                       F-1
4902-2237-l 120v5/202093-000 I


                                                                                                                       P266
         To facilitate subsequent transfers, all Bonds deposited by Direct Participants with DTC are registered in
the name of DTC's partnership nominee, Cede &Co. or such other name as requested by an authorized
representative ofDTC. The deposit of the Bonds with DTC and their registration in the name of Cede & Co. or
such other DTC nominee do not affect any change in beneficial ownership. DTC has no knowledge of the actual
Beneficial Owners of the Bonds; DTC's records reflect only the identity of the Direct Participants to whose
accounts such Bonds are credited, which may or may not be the Beneficial Owners. The Direct or Indirect
Participants will remain responsible for keeping account of their holdings on behalf of their customers.

         Conveyance of notices and other communications by DTC to Direct Participants, by Direct Participants
to Indirect Participants, and by Direct Pruiicipants and Indirect Participants to Beneficial Owners will be
governed by arrangements among them, subject to any statutory or regulatory requirements as may be in effect
from time to time. Beneficial Owners of Bonds may wish to take certain steps to augment the transmission to
them of notices of significant events with respect to the Bonds, such as tenders, defaults, and proposed
amendments to the Bonds documents. For example, Beneficial Owners of the Bonds may wish to asce11ain that
the nominee holding the Bonds for their benefit has agreed to obtain and h·ansmit notices to Beneficial Owners.
In the alternative, Beneficial Owners may wish to provide their names and addresses to the Paying Agent and
request that copies of notices be provided directly to them.

         Neither DTC nor Cede & Co. (nor such other DTC nominee) will consent or vote with respect to the
Bonds unless authorized by a Direct Pruticipant in accordance with DTC's MMI Procedmes. Under its usual
procedures, DTC mails an Omnibus Proxy to the District as soon as possible after the record date. The Omnibus
Proxy assigns Cede & Co. 's consenting or voting rights to those Direct Participants to whose accounts the Bonds
are credited on the record date (identified in a listing attached to the Omnibus Proxy).

         Payments of principal of and interest on_the Bonds will be made to Cede & Co., or such other nominee
as may be requested by an authorized representative of DTC. DTC's practice is to credit Direct Pa11icipants'
accounts upon DTC's receipt of funds and corresponding detail information from the District or the Paying
Agent, on payable date in accordance with their respective holdings shown on DTC's records. Payments by
Paiiicipants to Beneficial Owners will be governed by standing instructions and customary practices, as is the
case with securities held for the accounts of customers in beru·er form or registered in "street naine," and will be
the responsibility of such Pru"ticipant and not ofDTC, the Paying Agent or the District, subject to any statut01y
or regulatory requirements as may be in effect from time to time. Payment of principal and interest to Cede &
Co. (or such other nominee as may be requested by an authorized representative ofDTC) is the responsibility of
the Paying Agent, disbursement of such payments to Direct Participants will be the responsibility of DTC, and
disbursement of such payments to the Beneficial Owners will be the responsibility of Direct and Indil·ect
Pai1icipants.

        DTC may discontinue providing its service as depository with respect to the Bonds at any time by giving
reasonable notice to the Dish·ict or the Paying Agent. Under such circumstances, in the event that a successor
depository is not obtained, Bond certificates are required to be printed and delivered.

        The District may decide to discontinue use of the system of book-Entry Only transfers through DTC (or
a successor securities depository). In that event, the Bond certificates will be printed and delivered to DTC.

        The information in this section concerning DTC and DTC's book-enhy system has been obtained from
sources that the Dish·ict believes to be reliable, but the Dish'ict takes no responsibility for the accuracy thereof.

         In the event that (a) DTC determines not to continue to act as securities depository for the Bonds, or (b)
the District determines that DTC shall no longer act and delivers a written certificate to the Paying Agent to that
effect, then the Dish·ict will discontinue the Book-Enhy System with DTC for the Bonds. If the Dish'ict
determines to replace DTC with another qualified securities depository, the Dish'ict will prepare or direct the
preparation ofa new single separate, fully registered Bond for each maturity of the Bonds registered in the name
ofsuch successor or substitute securities depository as are not inconsistent with the terms ofthe Bond Resolution.

name or names the incumbent securities depository or its nominee transferring or exchanging the Bonds shall

        In the event that the Book-Entry System is discontinued, the following provisions would also apply: (i)
the Bonds will be made available in physical form, (ii) payment of principal of and interest on the Bonds will be

the Bonds will be transferable and exchangeable as provided in the Bond Resolution.

          The District and the Paying Agent do not have any responsibility or obligation to DTC Participants, to
 the persons for whom they act as nominees, to Beneficial Owners, or to any other person who is not sh~wn on

 by DTC or any DTC Participants; {ii} the payment by DTC or any DTC Participant ofany amount in respect of
 the principal ofand interest on the Bonds; (iii} the delive,y ofany notice which is permilled or required to be

 to the Bonds or any error or delay relating thereto.

                   RESOLUTION OF THE BOARD OF DIRECTORS OF THE
                SACRAMENTO METROPOLITAN FIRE DISTRICT APPROVING A
                             DISCLOSURE PROCEDURE

       RESOLVED, by the Board of Directors (the "Board of Directors") of the Sacramento
  Metropolitan Fire District (the "District"), as follows:

       WHEREAS, the District is an independent special district organized and existing
  under the laws of the State of California;

        WHEREAS, the District periodically sells securities of various types (the
  "Obligations") in order to finance or refinance capital improvements or for cash flow
  purposes;

        WHEREAS, the Board of Directors, the Fire Chief, the Chief Financial Officer and
  other District officials have previously been advised by the District's bond and disclosure
  counsel of the responsibilities of the members of the Board of Directors and of the District
  under federal securities laws when a public offering of Obligations is made by the District;

        WHEREAS, the Board of Directors, the Fire Chief, the Chief Financial Officer and
  other District officials understand that in offering the Obligations to the public, the District
  must comply with the "anti-fraud rules" of federal securities laws under section 17 of the
  Securities Act of 1933 and section 10(b) of the Securities and Exchange Act of 1934 (the
  "1934 Act"), and the regulations adopted by the Securities and Exchange Commission
  under those laws, particularly "Rule 10b-5" adopted under the 1934 Act.

       WHEREAS , in connection with the issuance of certain of its Obligations the District
  has executed continuing disclosure certificates and/or agreements (collectively, the
  "Agreements") pursuant to Rule 15c2-12 adopted by the Securities and Exchange
  Commission under the 1934 Act;

        WHEREAS, the Agreements require the District or its dissemination agent to file
  ·annual reports and notices of certain enumerated events;

         WHEREAS, in connection with its issuance of Obligations and the delivery of the
  Agreements, the District has put in place various policies, practices and procedures in an
  effort to ensure compliance with the anti-fraud rules of federal securities laws and its
  obligations under the Agreements;

        WHEREAS, the District now desires to memorialize those practices and
  procedures, in writing, and to add certain additional procedures in the Disclosure
  Procedures set forth in Exhibit A hereto (the "Disclosure Procedures"), to ensure ongoing
  compliance by District personnel with the District's responsibilities under federal securities
  laws;
                              Serving Sacramento and Placer Counties                                     P271
RESOLUTION NO. 2025-XXX

Sacramento Metropolitan Fire District, as follows:
